<div id="review_1332335488" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1332335488" />
    <a title="Ferris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/819818-ferris"><img alt="Ferris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207863553p2/819818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1332335488?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferris" href="/user/show/819818-ferris">Ferris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1332335488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4526735600429776832">Undoubtedly one of the best novels I have ever read about the duality of the human spirit. This novel reached into my heart and mind and drew me into its mystical, magical, superstitious Brazilian tale. Jorge Amado starts by tickling the reader's fancy with a romance between a good girl, Flor, and a lovable, sensual gambler, Vadinho. He is the classic villain we hate to love. That is the skeleton of the story. <br/><br/>Amado proceeds to people the Bahian city with fantastic and fantastical characters. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText4526735600429776832" style="display:none">Undoubtedly one of the best novels I have ever read about the duality of the human spirit. This novel reached into my heart and mind and drew me into its mystical, magical, superstitious Brazilian tale. Jorge Amado starts by tickling the reader's fancy with a romance between a good girl, Flor, and a lovable, sensual gambler, Vadinho. He is the classic villain we hate to love. That is the skeleton of the story. <br /><br />Amado proceeds to people the Bahian city with fantastic and fantastical characters. The reader meets the literati, the illiterate, the pagan and the prudish, the rich and the poor,the gossips, the whores, the matriarchs and more. Eventually, the reader finds it harder and harder to surface for air. All the while, Amado, while weaving a marvelous, prototypical Brazilian melodrama, is laying the complex groundwork for what I consider to be the primary theme of the novel. Just when I thought I was in the groove of the story of duality within our protagonist, Flor, Amado's tale erupts in primordial chaos of mind, body, and spirit. Mystical upheaval ensues as the gods become transparent in their own duplicity. Social class inequity, personal destiny, loyalty and love.....no topic remains off limits in this sweeping psychological story. Amado is an absolute master in his ability to create a culture and to reel in the reader using hooks baited with marvelous plot, engaging prose, absolutely wonderful character development and more. <br /><br />In the end, what can one believe in? Peace comes with acceptance of duality? Or, as the final sentences purports, "And with this we come to the end of the tale of Dona Flor and her two husbands, set forth in all its details ad mysteries, as clear and dark as life itself. All this took place in Bahia, where these and other acts of magic occur without startling anybody. If anyone has his doubts, let him ask Cardoso e Sa., and he will tell him whether or no it is the truth. He can be found on the planet Mars or on any poor corner of the city.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4526735600429776832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2524477979" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2524477979" />
    <a title="Leila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86535390-leila"><img alt="Leila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558307701p2/86535390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2524477979?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leila" href="/user/show/86535390-leila">Leila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2524477979&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2524477979" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer56500915172291241">Interesting and colourful narrative of a young woman's struggle to come to terms with her sexuality in the midst of a very conservative, religious (and hypocritical) Bahian society.<br/>In my view, the book is made of three parts: first part is husband Vadinho, which represents Eros love but also a life of poverty, neglect and betrayal; the second part relates to husband Teodoro, which stands for cast love and integrity, but also monotony and boredom; and finally the third part - Dona Flor's inner st</span>
  <span id="freeText56500915172291241" style="display:none">Interesting and colourful narrative of a young woman's struggle to come to terms with her sexuality in the midst of a very conservative, religious (and hypocritical) Bahian society.<br />In my view, the book is made of three parts: first part is husband Vadinho, which represents Eros love but also a life of poverty, neglect and betrayal; the second part relates to husband Teodoro, which stands for cast love and integrity, but also monotony and boredom; and finally the third part - Dona Flor's inner struggle to live with both husbands, symbolising her battle to overcome a repressed sexuality and free herself from society's stigma of women's sexual desire.<br />The last few chapters contain a very colourful battle of the heaven gods for Vadinho's soul, which represents Flor's internal conflict (and triumph) over her shame in longing for a kind of sex that was deemed dirty and unbecoming of a good and respecting wife.<br />Instead of public disgrace and moral regret, allowing herself to fantasise, masturbate and encourage her new husband Teodoro to innovate in their sexual routine (all because of her metaphorical giving-in to Vadinho's ghostly sexual advances) actually resulted in a much happier and fulfilled Dona Flor.<br />It took me a while to get to this conclusion but, in my opinion, the real message of the book is to be true to ourselves, and realise that often the voice in our heads holding us back from happiness is nothing more than our own projection of how we think society would judge us for our fantasies. </span>
  <a data-text-id="56500915172291241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3046467872" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3046467872" />
    <a title="Jennifer Pletcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39425019-jennifer-pletcher"><img alt="Jennifer Pletcher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421879143p2/39425019.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3046467872?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Pletcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Pletcher" href="/user/show/39425019-jennifer-pletcher">Jennifer Pletcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3046467872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1111858426914800946">This is the story of Dona Flor. Her husband, Vadinho, has just died. According to everyone around him - he was a gambling, good for nothing, and no one was surprised to see him go. After his death, Dona throws herself into her work and her life around her. Soon - she is being encouraged to remarry. She meets a pharmacist who is everyting Vadinho wasn't and soon they are married. However - after their marrige, she finds herself dreaming of Vadinho. Her new husband doesn't have Vadinho's passions,</span>
  <span id="freeText1111858426914800946" style="display:none">This is the story of Dona Flor. Her husband, Vadinho, has just died. According to everyone around him - he was a gambling, good for nothing, and no one was surprised to see him go. After his death, Dona throws herself into her work and her life around her. Soon - she is being encouraged to remarry. She meets a pharmacist who is everyting Vadinho wasn't and soon they are married. However - after their marrige, she finds herself dreaming of Vadinho. Her new husband doesn't have Vadinho's passions, and soon his ghost returns to her to bring excitement back to her life.<br /><br /><br /><br />This book was really hard to read. I am not a huge fan of magical realism, yet I keep finding these books regardless. I trudged through it, but it took forever for me to get into it and finish it. It was a bit of a chore to read. The second half was better thant the first, so I am glad I stuck with it. But a book that takes 300 pages to get me interested isn't something I would recommend.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3195834264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3195834264" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52482799-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3195834264?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/52482799-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3195834264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13240399797926582963">Dona Flor and Her Two Husbands is the definition of a slow-burner. This ponderous novel takes its time, waits for its moments and pounces hard to deliver intentions - the moral, sexual and social enlightenment/liberation/definition of a young Brazilian woman from Salvador, Dona Flor. It is an expert character study, not just of the sympathetic Dona Flor, but also of her two differing husbands, Cassonova-type Vadinho and straight laced Teodoro. It is encased in a social and human portrait of urba</span>
  <span id="freeText13240399797926582963" style="display:none">Dona Flor and Her Two Husbands is the definition of a slow-burner. This ponderous novel takes its time, waits for its moments and pounces hard to deliver intentions - the moral, sexual and social enlightenment/liberation/definition of a young Brazilian woman from Salvador, Dona Flor. It is an expert character study, not just of the sympathetic Dona Flor, but also of her two differing husbands, Cassonova-type Vadinho and straight laced Teodoro. It is encased in a social and human portrait of urban Salvador, painted in meticulous and often dull detail by the strong journalistic narrator with a particularly love of gossip. The social tapestry of Dona Flor's life is woven together by hundreds of characters, most of them superfluous to the plot, from her family to the gambling crews of Vadinho, her lady neighbours and friends, from the mystics, priests and soothsayers of the church and candomble rituals that inform the neighbourhood's lives to the students of Dona Flor's cookery school, from the artists and musicians of the street to the seriously toned members of Teodoro's amateur orchestra. No words are spared in this expansive and relentless description of Salvadorian society in the 1960s.<br /><br />After a obscure and jolting beginning that focusses on the events surrounding Vadinho's funeral, the book falls into the tone and patterns of an English society romance - Middlemarch transformed with a Brazilian carnival feel and oozing sexuality. Amado recalls the events leading up to Vadinho's marriage, their elopement, Flor's sexual awakening, her mother's hatred of her new, charming husband, and then the roller coaster of passion and pain that is her seven year relationship with compulsive gambler, drinker and romancer Vadinho. Amada repeats himself almost to the point of being painful - with Vadinho it's his scar from a knife fight, for example, with Teodoro it's the timetable of sexual intercourse on Wednesday and Saturdays (with an encore). It serves to give the book a light tone of mockery and humour but also reforces the fact that this is Flor's novel and it is her emotions and thoughts we are analysing. Amado expresses her stream of consciousness, the repetitive arguments she uses to justify her love for Vadinho, despite the fact he hits her and gambles away her money, and later to justify her sadness as a widow and her frustration with Teodoro's orderly, dull existence.<br /><br />This continues during the second third - the description of her second marriage to the druggist, Dr Teodoro, an upstanding citizen and a "perfect" husband. There is a clear comparison. Teodoro and Vadinho are deliberately opposite, almost ridiculously so. Yet they both restrict her with their own form of tyranny. Vadinho leaves her lonely and jealous, as well as financially precarious. Teodoro offers her security yet wishes to take away her autonomy by asking her to give us her cooking school. The battle Flor undergoes is one of freedom and identity. She defines herself by her marriages and yet they also hold her back. The most important comparions is that of her sex life with these too drastically different men. The sex scenes are brilliantly and vividly described by Amado - here is repetitions drill home the sexual personalities of the two men, their confidences and their insecurities. Flor is the one who develops, who yearns, who grows. The differing sexuality of the two husbands is wrapped up in their musical identities. Vadinho serenades her in the street like Romeo, Teodoro's amateur orchestra dedicates bassoon solos to her like an awfully staid scene of romance in a movie like the Sound of Music. Money is the other clash - Vadinho's throw away life (and death), Teodoro's need for security and safety built by his career, his property and his savings.<br /><br />After 400 pages carefully building these three character with patient realism (if sometimes a little comic and exaggerated) the final third of the books offers a much needed change of pace and tone. The dead Vadinho's return hints at magic realism but Dona Flor's story is more like a moral fairy tale by Oscar Wilde than a companion of South American realism. The repetitions and the descriptions of society are not abandonned; Amado's novel certainly takes a lot of patience and trust that the time investment will pay off. But it does pay off. In the end it is Flor that stands centre stage. The final pages are intense, sensual and very surprising. In the end it is not about who Flor will choose; the passion of Vadinho or the security of Teodoro. It's about defining herself apart from these two huge influences, from their repressive male personalities. Dona Flor is a fantastic creation, a colourful and complicated picture of female liberation and repression set in a buzzing social world of music, money, damaging gossips, African Gods, fortune telling, aspiring careers and ambitious musicians, gamblers and prostitutes, politicians and societies of dry pharmacists.<br /><br />And in the centre Flor, flavoured with the ingredients and aromas of Salvador that flow from her kitchen, where she is a teacher, independent of her husbands, blending into the urban environment that owns her and is informed by her. Not for those with a short attention span, Dona Flor is a long, patient, intelligent, funny and sensual novel that rewards readers with a brilliantly querky and inventive finale. 7</span>
  <a data-text-id="13240399797926582963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

O título refere-se às personagens femininas do romance, duas meias-irmãs órfãs, Margarida e Clara, de personalidades opostas, adotadas pelo Reitor. Disponível na www: \n\nAs Pupilas do Senhor Reitor, de Júlio Dinis, primeiro romance português do século, publicado inicialmente em 1866 em forma de folhetim, [...] só no ano seguinte apareceria em livro. [O s]eu caráter moralizador e a religiosidade que perpassa por todo o romance, a bondade capaz de chegar a extremos quase incríveis de sacrifício pessoal, são alguns dos ingredientes que transformaram em muito pouco tempo o autor desconhecido em sucesso nacional. \n\nA calma da cidade do interior (Ovar - Portugal) e a observação da vida simples das pessoas da aldeia propiciaram o aparecimento desse romance que, algum tempo depois, se tornaria um dos mais famosos em Portugal.\n\nOs capítulos são tipicamente folhetinescos: unidades narrativas com peripécias e final em suspensão. É um romance [que] está cheio de ironias bem humoradas, tornando-o, apesar do moralismo intencional, de leitura mais agradável.\n\nComo costuma acontecer com escritores românticos, Júlio Dinis também vê o mundo com as lentes do maniqueísmo. Assim, assenta sua obra em um jogo contínuo de oposições. Clara e Margarida, ambas generosas e de bom espírito, encaram a vida por forma diferente: a primeira, expansiva e alegre, por vezes estouvada, feliz de si própria e dos outros; a segunda, fechada numa reserva natural, dominando as reminiscências dum idílio infantil com Daniel na discrição e na saudade das horas idas - sentimento bem português de que neste livro se faz, por vezes, uma síntese Chirù, detentore di una giovinezza senza più innocenza, farà suo ogni insegnamento in modo spietato, regalando a Eleonora una lezione difficile da dimenticare.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/52683.Jorge_Amado">About Jorge Amado</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15429308427978170949">Jorge Amado de Faria was a Brazilian writer of the Modernist school. He was the best-known of modern Brazilian writers, his extensive work having been translated into some 30 languages and popularized in film, notably <i>Dona Flor and her Two Husbands,</i> (in Portuguese, <i>Dona Flor e Seus Dois Maridos</i>) in 1978. His work dealt largely with the poor urban black and mulatto communities of Bahia.<br/>(Wikipedia)<br/><br/>J</span>
  <span id="freeText15429308427978170949" style="display:none">Jorge Amado de Faria was a Brazilian writer of the Modernist school. He was the best-known of modern Brazilian writers, his extensive work having been translated into some 30 languages and popularized in film, notably <i>Dona Flor and her Two Husbands,</i> (in Portuguese, <i>Dona Flor e Seus Dois Maridos</i>) in 1978. His work dealt largely with the poor urban black and mulatto communities of Bahia.<br />(Wikipedia)<br /><br />Jorge Leal Amado de Faria (Itabuna, 10 de agosto de 1912 — Salvador, 6 de agosto de 2001) foi um dos mais famosos e traduzidos escritores brasileiros de todos os tempos. Integrou os quadros da intelectualidade comunista brasileira desde o final da primeira metade do século XX - ideologia presente em várias obras, como a retratação dos moradores do trapiche baiano em Capitães da Areia, de 1937.<br /><br />Jorge é o autor mais adaptado do cinema, do teatro e da televisão. Verdadeiros sucessos como Dona Flor e Seus Dois Maridos, Tenda dos Milagres, Tieta do Agreste, Gabriela, Cravo e Canela e Tereza Batista Cansada de Guerra foram criações suas. A obra literária de Jorge Amado – 49 livros, ao todo – também já foi tema de escolas de samba por todo o País. Seus livros foram traduzidos em 80 países, em 49 idiomas, bem como em braille e em fitas gravadas para cegos.<br /><br />Jorge foi superado, em número de vendas, apenas por Paulo Coelho. Mas em seu estilo - o romance ficcional -, não há paralelo no Brasil. Em 1994, a sua obra foi reconhecida com o Prêmio Camões.<br /><br />(wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15429308427978170949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
